0399215921|4.0|Eric Carle, best known for his breakout entomological bildungsroman, The Very Hungry Caterpillar, seems to present a simple parable about the value of hard work in his 1984 picture book, The Very Busy Spider.  But in contrast to The Very Hungry Caterpillar's universal themes of youthful overindulgence and transcendent maturity, The Very Busy Spider, when examined in its sociopolitical context, captures the zeitgeist of Ronald Reagan's \\"Morning in America,\\" reflecting both the optimism and ambivalence of the era.<br /><br />The protagonist of the story, an industrious female spider who floats onto a farmyard on a breeze from parts unknown, sets out to spin the web that will be both her home and her means of securing sustenance.  The spinning process is threatened, however, by interruptions from a parade of gadabout farm animals who try to tempt her with offers to \\"go for a ride,\\" \\"jump on the rocks,\\" or \\"roll in the mud.\\"  Our workaholic heroine studiously ignores these wastrels, refusing to even acknowledge them with a word of greeting.<br /><br />*Spoiler Alert* Finally, after the web is finished and the distractions have abated, she traps a fly, presumably devours it (although no illustrations support this assumption), and then goes to sleep.  Her final visitor, an owl, swoops down to admire her web, but she is too exhausted to bask in his praise.<br /><br />As a comment on the American public's growing animosity toward the welfare state in the eighties, The Very Busy Spider could not be any more succinct.  In a series of eight one-sided conversations in which each animal entreats the spider to join it on some sort of pointless lark and the spider does not answer because she is \\"very busy spinning her web,\\" Carle suggests that independent entrepreneurs, whose success depends on their willingness to make sacrifices and work past the point of physical fatigue to gain their economic foothold, feel they can brook no interference from those who while away their lives at the expense of the state.  The spider can ill afford to pass the time with these domesticated beasts lest she be brought down to their level: feeding at the trough of the farmer, wallowing in their own filth, shirking responsibility whenever possible, and ultimately being led to slaughter.<br /><br />When the only other undomesticated animal in the story, the owl, admires the spider's craftsmanship and work ethic, the spider's unawareness of the compliment bespeaks a quiet dignity inherent in those who make their own way in the world.  They don't need to talk about their achievements because they are too busy building upon them.<br /><br />But The Very Busy Spider is no unequivocal celebration of the entrepreneurial spirit.  The spider is, by her own choosing, an isolated figure in the barnyard community; and nowhere is this more apparent than in the last image in the book.  Her web, the product of her expertise and industry, is the farmyard equivalent of the glass and steel monuments to obsessive acquisition that glitter in the Manhattan skyline.  And yet the spider herself is barely visible, tucked away in a remote corner of her own creation, a solitary figure converting the victim of her latest exploit into the energy she will need to destroy more like him the next day.  It may be just a matter of hours until \\"Morning in America,\\" but Carle leaves us wondering if the self-sufficient spider really has much more to look forward to than the barnyard drones she so disdains.ate. B0043URV8M|2.0|Perhaps I should start placing blame with David Yates for the continued downward trend with regards to the music of Harry Potter.  Since his installment as director, the music has been severely lacking in almost every way imaginable.  Although many disagree, John Williams did a wonderful job at the outset of the series to create an unmistakable identity that has finally been utterly, entirely and unapologetically abandoned.  Patrick Doyle's work for the fourth was incredibly romantic, emotionally arresting and despite mostly disregarding prior themes, distinctive.  Since his work, the series has been progressively less interesting.    I initially lambasted Hooper's score for not fitting within the context of the Harry Potter universe.  Hooper's work lacked the depth and texture (attributable to the orchestration and ensemble, which was simply too miniscule for such an endeavor) necessary to capture the the essence of all that is Harry Potter.  Those criticisms remain true, but I have more recently come to the realization that while the music was fairly ill-fitting, it at least had a bit of character and originality.    Deathly Hallows Pt. 1 is not a bad score, but it is nothing if not entirely anonymous.  \\"Obliviate\\" is probably the most memorable track on the album presentation, and when one considers that relative to \\"Hedwig's Theme,\\" \\"The Chamber of Secrets,\\" \\"Buckbeak's Flight,\\" or \\"Golden Egg,\\" that is not an accolade.  As with many modern film scores, this music could be injected into any action-drama film and work fairly well, so my principle criticism of the music is the antithesis of what I disliked about Hooper's score(s): it does fit the context of the story and visuals well enough, but it delivers no emotional impact and will be completely forgotten within minutes.  John Williams is not the only composer capable of creating memorable themes or entire scores with effective and pervasive leitmotif (Patrick Doyle's theme for the family in Goblet of Fire isarguably one of the best in the entire series).  Although I freely admit that Desplat is not a favorite of mine, his work for The Golden Compass and The Twilight Saga: New Moon far eclipse his effort for Deathly Hallows.    Again, this music is not technically poor, but within the context of the film and the broader realm of movie scores, it is a missed opportunity of epic proportions.  This movie is the culmination (well, half) of a fictional event very dear to the hearts of hundreds of millions of people across the globe and it deserves a better musical accompaniment, one that is distinguishable and faithful to the series (by which I mean more than a three-second, studio-demanded reference to \\"Hedwig's Theme\\" on celesta).    I would assume it is safe to say that Desplat will be completing the series with Deathly Hallows Pt. 2 and I do not hestitate to express my disappointment.  I shall obviously reserve final judgement until the movie and score are released next year, but I am not hopeful that Desplat will finish the series in as such a wonderful way as it began.

B000000OMB|3.0|One of the more oddball shotgun marriages in musical history was Asia, a \\"supergroup\\" comprised of one ELP member (drummer Carl Palmer), one Crimson King (vocalist/bass John Wetton), a YesMan (guitarist Steve Howe) and a Buggle (keyboardist Geoff Downes). With that kind of pedigree, you would expect long, self-indulgent soloing and piles of classically inspired virtuosity. Surprisingly, none of that appeared on their Geffen debut in 1982.<br /><br />Instead, you got straight ahead arena rock with strong melodies and eager to please hooks on about half of the album. Yes, the album was stunningly well produced and played with verve, but these men were most certainly no pop=musicians. Downes might have been, considering the meager output of The Buggles, however it seems the man who wrote \\"Video Killed The Radio Star\\" really longed to write \\"Starship Trooper.\\" He and Wetton co-wrote the bulk of the songs here, and while there's plenty of soloing going on, it's economical. (Which was the alleged reason Howe left after two albums, he wanted more.)<br /><br />Again, though, these men are not pop writers. Which means that half the songs on \\"Asia\\" are utterly awful, long forgotten in the ongiong classic rock play for \\"Heat Of The Moment,\\" \\"Only Time Will Tell\\" and \\"Wildest Dreams.\\" Palmer gets to wail away on the drums during \\"Wildest Dreams,\\" while it is Downes' keyboards that make the album sound like the band didn't just drop in from the seventies. That didn't stop \\"Asia\\" from hitting number one and getting a lot of pretentious \\"serious music\\" snobs from creaming themselves over what they considered a rabbit punch to new wave. (Or critics from mocking the band for sounding like throwbacks IN the peak of new wave.)<br /><br />Either way, Asia had the last laugh. Some 40 years later, the good stuff on the disc sounds cool, but that means you're probably just as well off with [[ASIN:B0009NZ3XW Asia Gold]] - which at least has a more up-to-date track mastering - than just buying this one.ing this one.

B000W4D94I|5.0|When this movie came out as one of the earlier Dolby 5.1 DVD titles in 1990s, it was the showcase movie - or the benchmark - for which 5.1 is utilized. You can actually feel you are in the middle of the tornado the way the sound was decoded and played throughout the 5 speakers.    This blu-ray's surround sound still hold that gold standard in my opinion. It really utilize the two rear speakers well for 1) for objects flying from front to back and vice versa, and 2) within vicinity of the tornado when debris hit on your behind as well as the wind noises. The best part is you can hear transition of a debris flying from one speaker to the next, which gives the inside-the-tornado effect. This is something I find still difficult to realize with other modern titles, especially action films. They might have lots of explosions and clearer sounds, but never got that extensive directional feel.    As for the picture, it's still clear but obviously softer then the recent movies. But still can see edges of the hail (larger ice balls), and slanted rains. Most of the skin details of the actors can be seen pretty well also.    For the price of this blu-ray (around $10), it's a great get to add to collection. I would still use this movie to show case surround sound more than any other recent action movies.

B002MQYOFW|5.0|\\"The Hunger Games\\" is the first book of a trilogy by Suzanne Collins. This Kindle e-book edition is 384 pages in length.<br /><br />*SPOILER*<br /><br />A draconian regime has come to rule what was once the USA; now a land now called Panem. The main area called the 'Capital' holds sway with an iron fist over 12 other lesser 'districts'. Once a year, in an attempt to provide 'amusement' for their own populous AND to bluntly remind the other districts just who is boss, they put on an event called, 'The Hunger Games'. Each one the other 12 districts MUST pick, by lottery, one boy and one girl between the ages of 11 and 18 to participate in this event. The 24 contestants (called 'tributes') are put into a huge open outdoor wilderness where they must their strength, cunning and whatever else they my find in this 'arena' to try to become the sole survivor of these 'games'. The winner, along with the inhabitants of his/her district, get to eat and live in relative splendor for the next year.<br /><br />This is the tale of two district 12 candidates...following a girl named Katniss and, to a lesser extent, a boy named Peeta. Katniss shows us numerous traits of her maturation process as she struggles throughout 'the games';  twinges of guilt, a faltering self-esteem, loyalty issues, nagging doubts about her sense of morality,  her untested survival abilities and most important...would she or could she, when the moment came, be able to kill another human being.  During the course of the tale, some of these traits are strengthened, some diminished...some for the better and some not. Needless to say, Katniss is a very interesting heroine.<br /><br />*END SPOILER*<br /><br />Although this work can easily be classified as young adult (YA), there are many elements within these pages to satisfy most adult readers. Collins has done a remarkable job in preventing this work from becoming a series of miraculous escapes and unbelievable occurrences...things that so often plague the YA genre of books.  I thought, on several occasions, that I'd figured out what would seem the most likely outcome of some decision made by Katniss or  Peeta, and not only was I wrong most of the time, but the way things actually went or turned out, was nearly always  better, than I'd foreseen.<br /><br />Also, after I'd read enough to get a gist of what was happening, I found the plot turned out to be much more complex than what I'd expected. I was very impressed with the skill with which Collins described and manipulated the 'situations' that Katniss and Peeta found themselves facing during the 'games'. Nothing seems to help develop (or crush) the sense of growing up like having to make important decisions...and several of the decisions that our two heroes had to make could only be described as agonizing.<br /><br />Conclusion:<br />An exciting page turner...well written, superbly paced and a plot exceeding well thought out and executed. Written using 2 teenagers as the main protagonists, giving it a YA feel, but the overall quality shines through and will satisfy most adult readers.  I look forward to book II.<br />5 Stars<br /><br />Ray Nicholson<br /><br />PS<br />Thanks to amazon friend Paul Weiss for bringing this fine book to my attention.casions, that I'd figured out what would seem the most likely outcome of some decision made by Katniss or  Peeta, and not only was I wrong most of the time, but the way things actually went or turned out, was nearly always  better, than I'd foreseen.    Also, after I'd read enough to get a gist of what was happening, I found the plot turned out to be much more complex than what I'd expected. I was very impressed with the skill with which Collins described and manipulated the 'situations' that Katniss and Peeta found themselves facing during the 'games'. Nothing seems to help develop (or crush) the sense of growing up like having to make important decisions...and several of the decisions that our two heroes had to make could only be described as agonizing.    Conclusion:  An exciting page turner...well written, superbly paced and a plot exceeding well thought out and executed. Written using 2 teenagers as the main protagonists, giving it a YA feel, but the overall quality shines through and will satisfy most adult readers.  I look forward to book II.   5 Stars    Ray Nicholson    PS  Thanks to amazon friend Paul Weiss for bringing this fine book to my attention.

B003EV6DBM|3.0|Steven Moffatt, Doctor Who's new head writer, has won multiple awards writing for this show and worked with Spielberg, so he must be awesome, right?  Well, maybe not, as this season shows.  I enjoyed this season once through on BBC America, but when I watched the DVD, everything felt thin.  While I can't say this season is bad, it doesn't achieve the standards I've come to expect from Doctor Who.    On one hand, Matt Smith's Doctor isn't bad; he looks like an old, old man wearing a young man's body.  Karen Gillan as Amy makes a brash, outspoken foil to the Doctor, keeping him upright and challenging him to greater humanity.  Arthur Darvill's Rory, sadly underutilized, is pretty sharp, too.  And some of this year's villains, like Homo Reptilia or the Dream Lord, recapture the classic series' mystique and glory.    On the other hand, Smith waves the sonic screwdriver around so reflexively that it's like he has Tourette's.  Several episodes begin abruptly, with a problem or plot device that leaves writers' fingerprints on the stories, and end with long, talky denouements.  Moffatt's ambitious attempt to graft the season into one long arc, much like [[ASIN:B001K2KM9Q The Key to Time]], often seems strained, struggling to hang together against heavy-handed writing.    Many episodes, especially the climactic season-ender, only make sense if you make more leaps of faith than religion.  And I don't use that analogy lightly.  This season makes so many references to churches, bishops, and God that it starts to resemble evangelism.  I don't know if Moffatt and the writers' stable have a secret agenda, or just a pet issue, but whatever it is, they don't conceal it very well.    Don't misunderstand: this season isn't bad, nor do I fear it spells the end of Doctor Who.  I just had high hopes, and it doesn't withstand the scrutiny that comes with multiple viewings.  It has many good points, especially Smith himself, who reminds me of both Peter Davison and Paul McGann.  Perhaps things will improve.  After all, it took a year for Russell T. Davies to find his footing, too.

B002ZG980U|4.0|Going into Inception was kind of a gamble for me as my friends and colleagues apparently know me better than I thought.  I've been hearing this is the \\"one to check out\\" since the day it arrived in theaters this past summer and while my friends have once again proven spot-on with their understanding of the kind of fiction I enjoy, it is always worrisome when so much hype comes my way.  I have to make a very conscious effort not to get swept up in it, as that is the sure-fire way to become disappointed with any project (however ambitious).<br /><br />That said I was able to suppress my tendency to become the ultimate Inception fan boy until its home-disc release then quickly snatched up the Blu-ray with reservations on picking up a Leo DeCaprio T-shirt and matching coffee mug.  To cut the chase, I enjoyed this film quite a bit but perhaps not quite to the levels my buddies assumed I would and the reason for this stems from the fact that some of its finest conceptual moments have been done before in various mediums.  However, we'll get to all that.  For now, let us get to the nuts and bolts of the picture.<br /><br />Inception tells the tale of Dom Cobb (Leonardo DeCaprio), an Extractor (a man who enters people's dreams to gather information).  With a team of dream-experts consisting of Arthur (Joseph Gordon-Levitt), Ariadne (Ellen Page), Eames (Tom Hardy), and Yusuf (Dileep Rao), Cobb is hired by Saito (Ken Watanabe) to implant an idea (a unproven process called Inception) into the brain of Robert Fischer Jr. (Cillian Murphy).<br /><br />If this sounds a bit muddled to you, then perhaps steering very clear of this picture will be your best bet.  For all of its intricate explanations, Inception is a venerable swirl of intense sequences that demands multiple viewings if any coherency is to be established.<br /><br />I liken the initial viewing experience to the first time I went though the original Matrix (another Warner Brothers piece with similar themes): The line between reality and the construct is quite hazy and the laws separating them aren't quite clearly understood despite plenty of exposition attempting to force-feed them to you.<br /><br />I suppose in the interest of the average viewer's sanity, Christopher Nolan was forced to integrate a technique that I personally am not too fond of here in that when the situation demands it, suddenly the rules established earlier on are said not to apply \\"in this situation\\", hence upping the level of danger to the characters involved.  Again though, given the complexity of the material here, it is far more forgivable than if used in a more-linear piece.<br /><br />And speaking of linearity, I will continue to summarize the plot without giving away any real spoilers.  Cobb could well be the ultimate mind thief except for one minor annoyance: The appearance of Mal (Marion Cotillard) who tends to turn up in Cobb's dreams and jobs, botching them out of spite.  Additionally we learn that Cobb is a proud daddy in the real world and that motivation to be reunited with his kids is the catalyst for his agreeing to such a risky assignment.<br /><br />In fact, so delicate is the act of incepting a persuasive motivation that it demands Cobb and company to enter into a dream within a dream within a dream! Each depth of the subconscious mind said to be far riskier, more unstable, and (what I found to be coolest of all) operating in its own relative speed.<br />The film is pretty stunning, visually, right from the onset with undeniable attention paid in the merging of gritty, moody dialog sequences with larger-than-life mind-bending effects.  Direction is spectacular here as well with camera moves and angles that dazzle continually.  Hans Zimmer's score is spot-on as well.  Truly from a technical standpoint, the piece is a stunner.<br /><br />The 2.5-hour runtime happens quickly and efficiently thanks to roller coaster pacing and a bit of disconnected editing work.<br /><br />In all, if having gotten this far in my review and aren't sure exactly how I feel about the piece, that's a pretty accurate observation.  Parts of this one are brilliant but unlike 1998's The Matrix or the 2005 novel Human Interface (both of which present some very similar mind-bending concepts), portions of Inception (especially the twist at the end) leave a flavor of forced existentialism.  There is potential for greatness here in every single moment of the picture but appreciation for it all takes time to settle in much in the same way one doesn't guzzle a bottle of fine wine.<br /><br />I suppose in conclusion the goal of science fiction cinema is to entertain and there is little doubt that Inception delivers in this regard.  It is certainly thought provoking, unique and technically impeccable.  I will conclude this review with a quote from the film that best describes the experience as a whole:  \\"Dreams feel real while we're in them. It's only when we wake up that we realize how things are actually strange.\\"e, that's a pretty accurate observation. There is potential for greatness here in every single moment of the picture but appreciation for it all takes time to settle in much in the same way one doesn't guzzle a bottle of fine wine.    I suppose in conclusion the goal of science fiction cinema is to entertain and there is little doubt that Inception delivers in this regard.  It is certainly thought provoking, unique and technically impeccable.  I will conclude this review with a quote from the film that best describes the experience as a whole:  \\"Dreams feel real while we're in them. It's only when we wake up that we realize how things are actually strange.\\"

1439107955|5.0|I'm halfway through, and cannot put it down. What fascinates me most is his dark, never overstated undertext: he's writing a history of experimentation on sick humans,<br />undertaken without informing many of them of the risks, benefits, & unknowns.  Seen another way, it's a history of medical hubris, mutilation and poisoning in the name of \\"saving\\" (usually prolonging by a few months) lives and advancing science. Mukherjee expresses empathy for suffering patients -- and the reader supplies his own feelings and questions - but some of the most driven scientists Mukherjee follows seem to lose sight of the human being whose body became a battleground in the war between cancer and the doctor.  But then, I'm not through yet, and I don't know if he addresses the alternative of simply accepting fate, making the last months as good as possible, and facing death. I liked finding out which cancers now have good treatment track records, and which ones remain so widely incurable that for the majority of sufferers, referral to hospice or palliative care may be more realistic and humane than aggressive treatment. (And in some cases, it even produces longer survival times.)<br />I'm happily chewing my way through. He''s a great writer and knows how to maintain drama and suspense, weaving together an enormous cast of characters without getting me lost. Never polemical. A masterwork.
